class="firstHeading" lang="en">Dylan Thomas</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Dylan_Thomas_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Dylan Thomas (disambiguation)">Dylan Thomas (disambiguation)</a>.</div> \n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n\n</p>\n<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold"><span class="fn">Dylan Thomas</span></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<a href="/wiki/File:Dylan_Thomas_photo.jpg" class="image" title="Thomas at the Gotham Book Mart,in New York City, 1952[1]"><img alt="A black and white photo of Thomas in a book shop, he is wearing a suit with a white spotted bow tie." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bc/Dylan_Thomas_photo.jpg/220px-Dylan_Thomas_photo.jpg" width="220" height="292" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/bc/Dylan_Thomas_photo.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="274" data-file-height="364" /></a><div>Thomas at the <a href="/wiki/Gotham_Book_Mart" title="Gotham Book Mart">Gotham Book Mart</a>,<br />in New York City, 1952<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em;padding-right:0.65em;">Born</th><td style="line-height:1.4em;">\nDylan Marlais Thomas<br /><span style="display:none">(<span class="bday">1914-10-27</span>)</span>27 October 1914<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclop\xc3\xa6dia_Britannica_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop\xc3\xa6dia_Britannica-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><br /><a href="/wiki/Uplands,_Swansea" title="Uplands, Swansea">Uplands, Swansea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glamorgan" style="display:none">(1953-11-09)</span> (aged&#160;39)<br /><a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, United States</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em;padding-right:0.65em;">Resting place</th><td style="line-height:1.4em;">\n<a href="/wiki/Laugharne" title="Laugharne">Laugharne</a>, Carmarthenshire, Wales</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em;padding-right:0.65em;">Occupation</th><td class="role" style="line-height:1.4em;">\nPoet and writer</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em;padding-right:0.65em;">Literary movement</th><td style="line-height:1.4em;">\n<a href="/wiki/Modernism" title="Modernism">Modernism</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em;padding-right:0.65em;">Spouse</th><td style="line-height:1.4em;">\n<a href="/wiki/Caitlin_Thomas" title="Caitlin Thomas">Caitlin Macnamara</a> (m. 1937\xe2\x80\x931953, his death)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em;padding-right:0.65em;">Children</th><td style="line-height:1.4em;">\nLlewelyn Edouard Thomas (1939\xe2\x80\x932000)<br /><a href="/wiki/Aeronwy_Thomas" title="Aeronwy Thomas">Aeronwy Bryn Thomas</a> (1943\xe2\x80\x932009)<br />Colm Garan Hart Thomas (1949\xe2\x80\x932012)</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Dylan Marlais Thomas</b> (27 October 1914&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 9 November 1953) was a <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh</a> poet and writer whose works include the poems "<a href="/wiki/Do_not_go_gentle_into_that_good_night" title="Do not go gentle into that good night">Do not go gentle into that good night</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/And_death_shall_have_no_dominion" title="And death shall have no dominion">And death shall have no dominion</a>"; the \'play for voices\' <i><a href="/wiki/Under_Milk_Wood" title="Under Milk Wood">Under Milk Wood</a></i>; and stories and radio broadcasts such as <i><a href="/wiki/A_Child%27s_Christmas_in_Wales" title="A Child&#39;s Christmas in Wales">A Child\'s Christmas in Wales</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Portrait_of_the_Artist_as_a_Young_Dog" title="Portrait of the Artist as a Young Dog">Portrait of the Artist as a Young Dog</a></i>. He became widely popular in his lifetime and remained so after his premature death at the age of 39 in New York City. By then he had acquired a reputation, which he had encouraged, as a "roistering, drunken and doomed poet".<sup id="cite_ref-WAEoW_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WAEoW-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thomas was born in <a href="/wiki/Swansea" title="Swansea">Swansea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, in 1914. An undistinguished pupil, he left school at 16 and became a journalist for a short time. Many of his works appeared in print while he was still a teenager; however, it was the publication in 1934 of "Light breaks where no sun shines" that caught the attention of the literary world. While living in London, Thomas met <a href="/wiki/Caitlin_Thomas" title="Caitlin Thomas">Caitlin Macnamara</a>, whom he married in 1937. In 1938 they moved to the Welsh fishing village of   <a href="/wiki/Laugharne" title="Laugharne">Laugharne</a> where from 1949 they settled permanently and brought up their three children.\n</p><p>Thomas came to be appreciated as a popular poet during his lifetime, though he found earning a living as a writer difficult. He began augmenting his income with reading tours and radio broadcasts. His radio recordings for the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> during the late 1940s brought him to the public\'s attention, and he was frequently used by the BBC as a populist voice of the literary scene.\n</p><p>Thomas first travelled to the United States in the 1950s. His readings there brought him a degree of fame, while his erratic behaviour and drinking worsened. His time in America cemented his legend, however, and he went on to record to <a href="/wiki/Gramophone_record" class="mw-redirect" title="Gramophone record">vinyl</a> such works as <i>A Child\'s Christmas in Wales</i>. During his fourth trip to New York in 1953, Thomas became gravely ill and fell into a coma, from which he never recovered. He died on 9 November 1953. His body was returned to Wales, where he was interred at the village churchyard in Laugharne on 25 November 1953.\n</p><p>Although Thomas wrote exclusively in the English language, he has been acknowledged as one of the most important Welsh poets of the 20th century. He is noted for his original, rhythmic and ingenious use of words and imagery. His position as one of the great modern poets has been much discussed, and he remains popular with the public.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_and_career">Life and career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life and career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dylan_thomas_birthplace.jpg" class="image"><img alt="On a hill street stands a two storied semi-detached house with bay windows to the front and a sloped tiled roof with a chimney." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Dylan_thomas_birthplace.jpg/170px-Dylan_thomas_birthplace.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Dylan_thomas_birthplace.jpg/255px-Dylan_thomas_birthplace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Dylan_thomas_birthplace.jpg/340px-Dylan_thomas_birthplace.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dylan_thomas_birthplace.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>5 Cwmdonkin Drive, <a href="/wiki/Swansea" title="Swansea">Swansea</a>, birthplace of Dylan Thomas</div></div></div>\n<p>Dylan Thomas was born on 27 October 1914 in <a href="/wiki/Swansea" title="Swansea">Swansea</a>, the son of Florence Hannah (<i>n\xc3\xa9e</i> Williams; 1882\xe2\x80\x931958), a <a href="/wiki/Seamstress" class="mw-redirect" title="Seamstress">seamstress</a>, and David John Thomas (1876\xe2\x80\x931952), a teacher. His father had a first-class honours degree in English from <a href="/wiki/University_College,_Aberystwyth" class="mw-redirect" title="University College, Aberystwyth">University College, Aberystwyth</a> and ambitions to rise above his position teaching English literature at the local <a href="/wiki/Grammar_school" title="Grammar school">grammar school</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Thomas had one sibling, Nancy Marles (1906\xe2\x80\x931953), who was eight years his senior.<sup id="cite_ref-ONDB_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ONDB-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The children spoke only English, though their parents were bilingual in English and Welsh, and David Thomas gave Welsh lessons at home. Thomas\'s father chose the name Dylan, which could be translated as "son of the sea", after <a href="/wiki/Dylan_ail_Don" title="Dylan ail Don">Dylan ail Don</a>, a character in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mabinogion" class="mw-redirect" title="The Mabinogion">The Mabinogion</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> His middle name, Marlais, was given in honour of his great-uncle, William Thomas, a <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian</a> minister and poet whose <a href="/wiki/Bardic_name" title="Bardic name">bardic name</a> was <a href="/wiki/William_Thomas_(Gwilym_Marles)" title="William Thomas (Gwilym Marles)">Gwilym Marles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ONDB_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ONDB-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CUP_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CUP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Dylan, pronounced <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:IPA_for_Welsh" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:IPA for Welsh">\xcb\x88</a> [\xcb\x88d\xc9\x99lan] (Dull-an) in Welsh, caused his mother to worry that he might be teased as the "dull one".<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> When he broadcast on <a href="/wiki/BBC_Regional_Programme" title="BBC Regional Programme">Welsh BBC</a>, early in his career, he was introduced using this pronunciation. Thomas favoured the Anglicised pronunciation and gave instructions that it should be Dillan <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/\xc9\x99n/: &#39;on&#39; in &#39;button&#39;">\xc9\x99n</span></span>/</a></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-ONDB_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ONDB-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reckless1_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reckless1-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The red-brick semi-detached house at <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.dylanthomasbirthplace.com">5 Cwmdonkin Drive</a> (in the respectable area of the <a href="/wiki/Uplands,_Swansea" title="Uplands, Swansea">Uplands</a>), in which Thomas was born and lived until he was 23, had been bought by his parents a few months before his birth.<sup id="cite_ref-CUP_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CUP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> His childhood featured regular summer trips to <a href="/wiki/Llanstephan" class="mw-redirect" title="Llanstephan">Llanstephan</a> where his maternal relatives were the sixth generation to farm there.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> His mother\'s family, the Williamses, lived in such farms as Waunfwlchan, Llwyngwyn, Maesgwyn and Penycoed.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The memory of Fernhill, a dairy farm owned by his maternal aunt, Ann Jones,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> is evoked in the 1945 lyrical poem "<a href="/wiki/Fern_Hill" title="Fern Hill">Fern Hill</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Thomas had <a href="/wiki/Bronchitis" title="Bronchitis">bronchitis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a> in childhood and struggled with these throughout his life. Thomas was indulged by his mother and enjoyed being mollycoddled, a trait he carried into adulthood, and he was skilful in gaining attention and sympathy.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris1989p25_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris1989p25-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Thomas\' formal education began at Mrs Hole\'s <a href="/wiki/Dame_school" title="Dame school">dame school</a>, a private school on Mirador Crescent, a few streets away from his home.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> He described his experience there in <i>Quite Early One Morning</i>:\n</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>Never was there such a dame school as ours, so firm and kind and smelling of galoshes, with the sweet and fumbled music of the piano lessons drifting down from upstairs to the lonely schoolroom, where only the sometimes tearful wicked sat over undone sums, or to repent a little crime&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 the pulling of a girl\'s hair during geography, the sly shin kick under the table during English literature.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dylan_Thomas_Theatre_Home_of_Swansea_Little_Theatre.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A wide three storied building with windows to the upper two stories and an entrance on the ground floor. A statue of Thomas sits outside." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Dylan_Thomas_Theatre_Home_of_Swansea_Little_Theatre.jpg/170px-Dylan_Thomas_Theatre_Home_of_Swansea_Little_Theatre.jpg" width="170" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Dylan_Thomas_Theatre_Home_of_Swansea_Little_Theatre.jpg/255px-Dylan_Thomas_Theatre_Home_of_Swansea_Little_Theatre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2c/Dylan_Thomas_Theatre_Home_of_Swansea_Little_Theatre.jpg 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="341" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dylan_Thomas_Theatre_Home_of_Swansea_Little_Theatre.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dylan_Thomas_Theatre" title="Dylan Thomas Theatre">Dylan Thomas Theatre</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In October 1925, Thomas enrolled at <a href="/wiki/Bishop_Gore_School#Dylan_Thomas_and_Bishop_Gore" title="Bishop Gore School">Swansea Grammar School</a> for boys, in <a href="/wiki/Mount_Pleasant,_Swansea" title="Mount Pleasant, Swansea">Mount Pleasant</a>, where his father taught English.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> He was an undistinguished pupil who shied away from school, preferring reading.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In his first year one of his poems was published in the school\'s magazine, and before he left he became its editor.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> During his final school years he began writing poetry in notebooks; the first poem, dated 27 April (1930), is entitled "Osiris, come to Isis".<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In June 1928 Thomas won the school\'s mile race, held at <a href="/wiki/St._Helen%27s_Rugby_and_Cricket_Ground" title="St. Helen&#39;s Rugby and Cricket Ground">St. Helen\'s Ground</a>; he carried a newspaper photograph of his victory with him until his death.<sup id="cite_ref-Dylan_Thomas_official_website_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dylan_Thomas_official_website-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In 1931, when he was 16, Thomas left school to become a reporter for the <i><a href="/wiki/South_Wales_Evening_Post" title="South Wales Evening Post">South Wales Daily Post</a></i>, only to leave under pressure<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> 18 months later.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Thomas continued to work as a freelance journalist for several years, during which time he remained at Cwmdonkin Drive and continued to add to his notebooks, amassing 200 poems in four books between 1930 and 1934. Of the 90 poems he published, half were written during these years.<sup id="cite_ref-ONDB_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ONDB-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In his free time, he joined the amateur dramatic group at the <a href="/wiki/Dylan_Thomas_Theatre" title="Dylan Thomas Theatre">Little Theatre</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mumbles" title="Mumbles">Mumbles</a>, visited the cinema in Uplands, took walks along <a href="/wiki/Swansea_Bay" title="Swansea Bay">Swansea Bay</a>, and frequented Swansea\'s <a href="/wiki/Pub" title="Pub">pubs</a>, especially the Antelope and the Mermaid Hotels in Mumbles.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Kardomah_Caf%C3%A9" class="mw-redirect" title="Kardomah Caf\xc3\xa9">Kardomah Caf\xc3\xa9</a>, close to the newspaper office in Castle Street, he met his creative contemporaries, including his friend the poet <a href="/wiki/Vernon_Watkins" title="Vernon Watkins">Vernon Watkins</a>. The group of writers, musicians and artists became known as "<a href="/wiki/The_Kardomah_Gang" title="The Kardomah Gang">The Kardomah Gang</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In 1933, Thomas visited London for probably the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1933.E2.80.931939"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1933\xe2\x80\x931939">1933\xe2\x80\x931939</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1933\xe2\x80\x931939">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Thomas was a teenager when many of the poems for which he became famous were published: "<a href="/wiki/And_death_shall_have_no_dominion" title="And death shall have no dominion">And death shall have no dominion</a>", "Before I Knocked" and "The Force That Through the Green Fuse Drives the Flower". "And death shall have no dominion" appeared in the <i><a href="/wiki/New_English_Weekly" class="mw-redirect" title="New English Weekly">New English Weekly</a></i> in May 1933.<sup id="cite_ref-ONDB_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ONDB-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> When "Light breaks where no sun shines" appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Listener_(magazine)" title="The Listener (magazine)">The Listener</a></i> in 1934, it caught the attention of three senior figures in literary London, <a href="/wiki/T._S._Eliot" title="T. S. Eliot">T. S. Eliot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Grigson" title="Geoffrey Grigson">Geoffrey Grigson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Spender" title="Stephen Spender">Stephen Spender</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CUP_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CUP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> They contacted Thomas and his first poetry volume, <i>18 Poems</i>, was published in December 1934. <i>18 Poems</i> was noted for its visionary qualities which led to critic <a href="/wiki/Desmond_Hawkins" title="Desmond Hawkins">Desmond Hawkins</a> writing that the work was "the sort of bomb that bursts no more than once in three years".<sup id="cite_ref-ONDB_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ONDB-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reckless2_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reckless2-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The volume was critically acclaimed and won a contest run by the <i><a href="/wiki/Sunday_Referee" title="Sunday Referee">Sunday Referee</a></i>, netting him new admirers from the London poetry world, including <a href="/wiki/Edith_Sitwell" title="Edith Sitwell">Edith Sitwell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Muir" title="Edwin Muir">Edwin Muir</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CUP_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CUP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The anthology was published by <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Caton" title="Reginald Caton">Fortune Press</a>, in part a vanity publisher that did not pay its writers and expected them to buy a certain number of copies themselves.  A similar arrangement was used by other new authors including <a href="/wiki/Philip_Larkin" title="Philip Larkin">Philip Larkin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In September 1935, Thomas met Vernon Watkins, thus beginning a lifelong friendship.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Thomas introduced Watkins, working at Lloyds Bank at the time, to his friends, now known as <a href="/wiki/The_Kardomah_Gang" title="The Kardomah Gang">The Kardomah Gang</a>. In those days, Thomas used to frequent the cinema on Mondays with Tom Warner who, like Watkins, had recently suffered a <a href="/wiki/Nervous_breakdown" class="mw-redirect" title="Nervous breakdown">nervous breakdown</a>. After these trips, Warner would bring Thomas back for supper with his aunt. On one occasion, when she served him a boiled egg, she had to cut its top off for him, as Thomas did not know how to do this. This was because his mother had done it for him all his life, an example of her coddling him.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Years later, his wife Caitlin would still have to prepare his eggs for him.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> In December 1935 Thomas contributed the poem "The Hand That Signed the Paper" to Issue 18 of the bi-monthly <i>New Verse</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In 1936, his next collection <i>Twenty-five Poems</i>, published by <a href="/wiki/J._M._Dent" title="J. M. Dent">J. M. Dent</a>, also received much critical praise.<sup id="cite_ref-CUP_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CUP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In all, he wrote half his poems while living at Cwmdonkin Drive before moving to London. It was the time that Thomas\'s reputation for heavy drinking developed.<sup id="cite_ref-Reckless2_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reckless2-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In early 1936, Thomas met <a href="/wiki/Caitlin_Thomas" title="Caitlin Thomas">Caitlin Macnamara</a> (1913\xe2\x80\x9394), a 22-year-old blonde-haired, blue-eyed dancer of Irish descent. She had run away from home, intent on making a career in dance, and aged 18 joined the chorus line at the <a href="/wiki/London_Palladium" title="London Palladium">London Palladium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris1989_151_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris1989_151-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Race_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Race-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cait_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cait-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Introduced by <a href="/wiki/Augustus_John" title="Augustus John">Augustus John</a>, Caitlin\'s lover, they met in The Wheatsheaf pub on Rathbone Place in London\'s <a href="/wiki/West_End_of_London" title="West End of London">West End</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris1989_151_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris1989_151-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cait_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cait-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Caitlin_obit_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caitlin_obit-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Laying his head in her lap, a drunken Thomas proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-Race_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Race-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Thomas liked to comment that he and Caitlin were in bed together ten minutes after they first met.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Although Caitlin initially continued her relationship with John, she and Thomas began a correspondence, and in the second half of 1936 were courting.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> They married at the register office in <a href="/wiki/Penzance" title="Penzance">Penzance</a>, Cornwall, on 11 July 1937.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In early 1938 they moved to Wales, renting a cottage in the village of <a href="/wiki/Laugharne" title="Laugharne">Laugharne</a>, Carmarthenshire.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Their first child, Llewelyn Edouard, was born on 30 January 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the late 1930s, Thomas was embraced as the "poetic herald" for a group of English poets, the <a href="/wiki/New_Apocalyptics" title="New Apocalyptics">New Apocalyptics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jackaman_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jackaman-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Thomas refused to align himself with them and declined to sign their manifesto. He later stated that he believed they were "intellectual muckpots leaning on a theory".<sup id="cite_ref-Jackaman_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jackaman-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Despite this, many of the group, including <a href="/wiki/Henry_Treece" title="Henry Treece">Henry Treece</a>, modelled their work on Thomas.<sup id="cite_ref-Jackaman_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jackaman-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Wartime.2C_1939.E2.80.931945"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wartime,_1939\xe2\x80\x931945">Wartime, 1939\xe2\x80\x931945</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Wartime, 1939\xe2\x80\x931945">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1939, a collection of 16 poems and seven of the 20 short stories published by Thomas in magazines since 1934, appeared as  <i>The Map of Love</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Ten stories in his next book, <i>Portrait of the Artist as a Young Dog</i> (1940), were based less on lavish fantasy than those in <i>The Map of Love</i> and more on real-life romances featuring himself in Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-ONDB_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ONDB-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Sales of both books were poor, resulting in Thomas living on meagre fees from writing and reviewing. At this time he borrowed heavily from friends and acquaintances.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Hounded by creditors, Thomas and his family left Laugharne in July 1940 and moved to the home of critic John Davenport in <a href="/wiki/Marshfield,_Gloucestershire" title="Marshfield, Gloucestershire">Marshfield, Gloucestershire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;nb 2&#93;</a></sup> There Thomas collaborated with Davenport on the satire <i>The Death of the King\'s Canary</i>, though due to fears of libel the work was not published until 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the outset of the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, Thomas was worried about conscription, and referred to his ailment as "an unreliable lung". Coughing sometimes confined him to bed, and he had a history of bringing up blood and mucus.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> After initially seeking employment in a <a href="/wiki/Reserved_occupation" title="Reserved occupation">reserved occupation</a>, he managed to be classified Grade III, which meant that he would be among the last to be called up for service.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;nb 3&#93;</a></sup> Saddened to see his friends going on active service, he continued drinking and struggled to support his family. He wrote begging letters to random literary figures asking for support, a plan he hoped would provide a long-term regular income.<sup id="cite_ref-ONDB_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ONDB-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Thomas supplemented his income by writing scripts for the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>, which not only gave him additional earnings but also provided evidence that he was engaged in essential war work.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In February 1941, <a href="/wiki/Swansea_Blitz" title="Swansea Blitz">Swansea was bombed</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a> in a "three nights\' blitz". Castle Street was one of many streets that suffered badly; rows of shops, including the Kardomah Caf\xc3\xa9, were destroyed. Thomas walked through the bombed-out shell of the town centre with his friend Bert Trick. Upset at the sight, he concluded: "Our Swansea is dead".<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Soon after the bombing raids, Thomas wrote a radio play, <i>Return Journey Home</i>, which described the caf\xc3\xa9 as being "razed to the snow".<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The play was first broadcast on 15 June 1947. The Kardomah Caf\xc3\xa9 reopened on Portland Street after the war.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In May 1941, Thomas and Caitlin left their son with his grandmother at <a href="/wiki/Blashford" title="Blashford">Blashford</a> and moved to London.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Thomas hoped to find employment in the film industry and wrote to the director of the films division of the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Information_(United_Kingdom)" title="Ministry of Information (United Kingdom)">Ministry of Information</a> (MOI).<sup id="cite_ref-ONDB_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ONDB-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> After being rebuffed, he found work with Strand Films, providing him with his first regular income since the <i>Daily Post</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Strand produced films for the MOI; Thomas scripted at least five films in 1942, <i>This Is Colour</i> (a history of the British dyeing industry) and <i>New Towns For Old</i> (on post-war reconstruction). <i>These Are The Men</i> (1943) was a more ambitious piece in which Thomas\'s verse accompanies <a href="/wiki/Leni_Riefenstahl" title="Leni Riefenstahl">Leni Riefenstahl</a>\'s footage of an early <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Rally" title="Nuremberg Rally">Nuremberg Rally</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;nb 4&#93;</a></sup> <i>Conquest of a Germ</i> (1944) explored the use of early antibiotics in the fight against <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>. <i>Our Country</i> (1945) was a romantic tour of Britain set to Thomas\'s poetry.<sup id="cite_ref-arts.guardian.co.uk_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arts.guardian.co.uk-68">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In early 1943, Thomas began a relationship with Pamela Glendower; one of several affairs he had during his marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian17-08-03_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian17-08-03-70">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The affairs either ran out of steam or were halted after Caitlin discovered his infidelity.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian17-08-03_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian17-08-03-70">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In March 1943 Caitlin gave birth to a daughter, <a href="/wiki/Aeronwy_Thomas-Ellis" class="mw-redirect" title="Aeronwy Thomas-Ellis">Aeronwy</a>, in London.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian17-08-03_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian17-08-03-70">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> They lived in a run-down studio in Chelsea, made up of a single large room with a curtain to separate the kitchen.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1944, with the threat of <a href="/wiki/V-1_flying_bomb" title="V-1 flying bomb">German flying bombs</a> on London, Thomas moved to the family cottage at Blaen Cwm near <a href="/wiki/Llangain" title="Llangain">Llangain</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> where Thomas resumed writing poetry, completing "Holy Spring" and "Vision and Prayer".<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In September Thomas and Caitlin moved to <a href="/wiki/New_Quay" title="New Quay">New Quay</a> in Cardiganshire (Ceredigion), which inspired Thomas to pen the radio piece <i>Quite Early One Morning</i>, a sketch for his later work, <i>Under Milk Wood</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris_1989_213_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris_1989_213-74">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Of the poetry written at this time, of note is "Fern Hill", believed to have been started while living in New Quay, but completed at Blaen Cwm in mid-1945.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris_1989,_p._214_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris_1989,_p._214-75">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;nb 5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Broadcasting_years_1945.E2.80.931949"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Broadcasting_years_1945\xe2\x80\x931949">Broadcasting years 1945\xe2\x80\x931949</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Broadcasting years 1945\xe2\x80\x931949">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Blick_auf_das_Dylan_Thomas_Boathouse_und_die_Trichterm%C3%BCndung_des_Taf,_Wales.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Blick_auf_das_Dylan_Thomas_Boathouse_und_die_Trichterm%C3%BCndung_des_Taf%2C_Wales.jpg/220px-Blick_auf_das_Dylan_Thomas_Boathouse_und_die_Trichterm%C3%BCndung_des_Taf%2C_Wales.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Blick_auf_das_Dylan_Thomas_Boathouse_und_die_Trichterm%C3%BCndung_des_Taf%2C_Wales.jpg/330px-Blick_auf_das_Dylan_Thomas_Boathouse_und_die_Trichterm%C3%BCndung_des_Taf%2C_Wales.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Blick_auf_das_Dylan_Thomas_Boathouse_und_die_Trichterm%C3%BCndung_des_Taf%2C_Wales.jpg/440px-Blick_auf_das_Dylan_Thomas_Boathouse_und_die_Trichterm%C3%BCndung_des_Taf%2C_Wales.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Blick_auf_das_Dylan_Thomas_Boathouse_und_die_Trichterm%C3%BCndung_des_Taf,_Wales.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Boat House, Laugharne, the Thomas family home from 1949</div></div></div>\n<p>Although Thomas had previously written for the BBC, it was a minor and intermittent source of income. In 1943 he wrote and recorded a 15-minute talk entitled "Reminiscences of Childhood" for the Welsh BBC. In December 1944 he recorded <i>Quite Early One Morning</i> (produced by <a href="/wiki/Aneirin_Talfan_Davies" title="Aneirin Talfan Davies">Aneirin Talfan Davies</a>, again for the Welsh BBC) but when Davies offered it for national broadcast BBC London turned it down.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris_1989_213_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris_1989_213-74">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> On 31 August 1945 the <a href="/wiki/BBC_Home_Service" title="BBC Home Service">BBC Home Service</a> broadcast <i>Quite Early One Morning</i>, and in the three years beginning October 1945, Thomas made over a hundred broadcasts for the corporation.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Thomas was employed not only for his poetry readings, but for discussions and critiques.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dylan_Thomas_Writing_Shed_(7896674354).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Dylan_Thomas_Writing_Shed_%287896674354%29.jpg/170px-Dylan_Thomas_Writing_Shed_%287896674354%29.jpg" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Dylan_Thomas_Writing_Shed_%287896674354%29.jpg/255px-Dylan_Thomas_Writing_Shed_%287896674354%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Dylan_Thomas_Writing_Shed_%287896674354%29.jpg/340px-Dylan_Thomas_Writing_Shed_%287896674354%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1936" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dylan_Thomas_Writing_Shed_(7896674354).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thomas\'s <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">\xc2\xa3</a>5 writing shed in Laugharne, Carmarthenshire</div></div></div>\n<p>By late September 1945 the Thomases had left Wales and were living with various friends in London.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The publication of <i><a href="/wiki/Deaths_and_Entrances" title="Deaths and Entrances">Deaths and Entrances</a></i> in 1946 was a turning point for Thomas. Poet and critic <a href="/wiki/Walter_J._Turner" title="Walter J. Turner">Walter J. Turner</a> commented in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Spectator" title="The Spectator">The Spectator</a></i>, "This book alone, in my opinion, ranks him as a major poet".<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the second half of 1945 Thomas began reading for the BBC Radio programme, <i>Book of Verse</i>, broadcast weekly to the Far East.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> This provided Thomas with a regular income and brought him into contact with <a href="/wiki/Louis_MacNeice" title="Louis MacNeice">Louis MacNeice</a>, a congenial drinking companion whose advice Thomas cherished.<sup id="cite_ref-Read_1964,_p._116_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Read_1964,_p._116-83">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> On 29 September 1946, the BBC began transmitting the <i><a href="/wiki/BBC_Third_Programme" title="BBC Third Programme">Third Programme</a></i>, a high-culture network which provided opportunities for Thomas.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> He appeared in the play <i>Comus</i> for the Third Programme, the day after the network launched, and his rich, sonorous voice led to character parts, including the lead in Aeschylus\' <i><a href="/wiki/Agamemnon_(play)" class="mw-redirect" title="Agamemnon (play)">Agamemnon</a></i> and Satan in an adaptation of <i><a href="/wiki/Paradise_Lost" title="Paradise Lost">Paradise Lost</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Read_1964,_p._116_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Read_1964,_p._116-83">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Thomas remained a popular guest on radio talk shows for the BBC, who regarded him as "useful should a younger generation poet be needed".<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> He had an uneasy relationship with BBC management and a staff job was never an option, with drinking cited as the problem.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Despite this, Thomas became a familiar radio voice and within Britain was "in every sense a celebrity".<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thomas visited the home of historian <a href="/wiki/A._J._P._Taylor" title="A. J. P. Taylor">A. J. P. Taylor</a> in <a href="/wiki/Disley" title="Disley">Disley</a>. Although Taylor disliked him intensely, he stayed for a month, drinking "on a monumental scale", up to 15 or 20 pints of beer a day. In late 1946 Thomas turned up at the Taylors\' again, this time homeless and with Caitlin. Margaret Taylor let them take up residence in the garden summerhouse.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In May 1949 Thomas and his family moved to his final home, the <a href="/wiki/Dylan_Thomas_Boathouse" title="Dylan Thomas Boathouse">Boat House</a> at Laugharne, purchased for him at a cost of \xc2\xa32,500 in April 1949 by Margaret Taylor.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Thomas acquired a garage a hundred yards from the house on a cliff ledge which he turned into his writing shed, and where he wrote several of his most acclaimed poems.<sup id="cite_ref-Writing_shed_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Writing_shed-91">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Just before moving into there, Thomas rented "Pelican House" opposite his regular drinking den, <a href="/wiki/Brown%27s_Hotel_(Laugharne)" title="Brown&#39;s Hotel (Laugharne)">Brown\'s Hotel</a>, for his parents<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> who lived there from 1949 until 1953. It was there that his father died and the funeral was held.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\nCaitlin gave birth to their third child, a boy named Colm Garan Hart, on 25 July 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="American_tours.2C_1950.E2.80.931953"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="American_tours,_1950\xe2\x80\x931953">American tours, 1950\xe2\x80\x931953</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: American tours, 1950\xe2\x80\x931953">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/John_Malcolm_Brinnin" title="John Malcolm Brinnin">John Brinnin</a> invited Thomas to New York, where in 1950 they embarked on a lucrative three-month tour of arts centres and campuses.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The tour, which began in front of an audience of a thousand at the Kaufmann Auditorium of the Poetry Centre in New York, took in about 40 venues.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;nb 6&#93;</a></sup> During the tour Thomas was invited to many parties and functions and on several occasions became drunk \xe2\x80\x93 going out of his way to shock people \xe2\x80\x93 and was a difficult guest.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Thomas drank before some of his readings, though it is argued<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (July 2015)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> he may have pretended to be more affected by it than he actually was.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The writer <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Hardwick_(writer)" title="Elizabeth Hardwick (writer)">Elizabeth Hardwick</a> recalled how intoxicating a performer he was and how the tension would build before a performance: "Would he arrive only to break down on the stage? Would some dismaying scene take place at the faculty party? Would he be offensive, violent, obscene?"<sup id="cite_ref-Reckless1_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reckless1-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Caitlin said in her memoir, "Nobody ever needed encouragement less, and he was drowned in it."<sup id="cite_ref-Reckless1_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reckless1-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:And_death_shall_have_no_dominion.ogg" title="File:And death shall have no dominion.ogg">And death shall have no dominion</a></div>\n<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="31.718458049887" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="And_death_shall_have_no_dominion.ogg" data-mwprovider="local"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/f1/And_death_shall_have_no_dominion.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (109 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="109084" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/transcoded/f/f1/And_death_shall_have_no_dominion.ogg/And_death_shall_have_no_dominion.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="115032" /></audio></div></div>\n<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">Thomas reads "And death shall have no dominion" for a 1953 recording</div></div></td></tr>\n<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>On returning to Britain Thomas began work on two further poems, "In the white giant\'s thigh", which he read on the <i>Third Programme</i> in September 1950, and the incomplete "In country heaven".<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> 1950 is also believed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (July 2015)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> to be the year that he began work on <i>Under Milk Wood</i>, under the working title \'The Town That Was Mad\'.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The task of seeing this work through to production was assigned to the BBC\'s <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Cleverdon" title="Douglas Cleverdon">Douglas Cleverdon</a>, who had been responsible for casting Thomas in \'Paradise Lost\'.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Despite Cleverdon\'s urges, the script slipped from Thomas\'s priorities and in early 1951 he took a trip to <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> to work on a film for the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Iranian_Oil_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Iranian Oil Company">Anglo-Iranian Oil Company</a>. The film was never made, with Thomas returning to Wales in February, though his time there allowed him to provide a few minutes of material for a BBC documentary entitled \'Persian Oil\'.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Early that year Thomas wrote two poems, which Thomas\'s principal biographer, Paul Ferris describes as "unusually blunt"; the ribald "Lament" and an ode, in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Villanelle" title="Villanelle">villanelle</a>, to his dying father "<a href="/wiki/Do_not_go_gentle_into_that_good_night" title="Do not go gentle into that good night">Do not go gentle into that good night</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite a range of wealthy patrons, including Margaret Taylor, Princess Marguerite <a href="/wiki/Caetani" title="Caetani">Caetani</a> and Marged Howard-Stepney, Thomas was still in financial difficulty, and he wrote several begging letters to notable literary figures including the likes of <a href="/wiki/T._S._Eliot" title="T. S. Eliot">T. S. Eliot</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Taylor was not keen on Thomas taking another trip to the United States, and thought that if Thomas had a permanent address in London he would be able to gain steady work there.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> She bought a property, 54 Delancey Street, in <a href="/wiki/Camden_Town" title="Camden Town">Camden Town</a>, and in late 1951 Thomas and Caitlin lived in the basement flat.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Thomas would describe the flat as his "London house of horror" and did not return there after his 1952 tour of America.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thomas undertook a second tour of the United States in 1952, this time with Caitlin \xe2\x80\x93 after she had discovered he had been unfaithful on his earlier trip.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> They drank heavily, and Thomas began to suffer with <a href="/wiki/Gout" title="Gout">gout</a> and <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_distress" class="mw-redirect" title="Respiratory distress">lung problems</a>. The second tour was the most intensive of the four, taking in 46 engagements.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The trip also resulted in Thomas recording his first poetry to vinyl, which <a href="/wiki/Caedmon_Audio" title="Caedmon Audio">Caedmon Records</a> released in America later that year.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> One of his works recorded during this time, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Child%27s_Christmas_in_Wales" title="A Child&#39;s Christmas in Wales">A Child\'s Christmas in Wales</a></i>, became his most popular prose work in America.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris_1989,_p._214_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris_1989,_p._214-75">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The original 1952 recording of <i>A Child\'s Christmas in Wales</i> was a 2008 selection for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Recording_Registry" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Recording Registry">United States National Recording Registry</a>, stating that it is "credited with launching the <a href="/wiki/Audiobook" title="Audiobook">audiobook</a> industry in the United States".<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In April 1953 Thomas returned alone for a third tour of America.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> He performed a "work in progress" version of <i>Under Milk Wood</i>, solo, for the first time at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> on 3 May.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> A week later the work was performed with a full cast at the Poetry Centre in New York. He met the deadline only after being locked in a room by Brinnin\'s assistant, Liz Reitell, and was still editing the script on the afternoon of the performance; its last lines were handed to the actors as they put on their makeup.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bold61_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bold61-118">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During this penultimate tour Thomas met the composer <a href="/wiki/Stravinsky" class="mw-redirect" title="Stravinsky">Igor Stravinsky</a> who had become an admirer after been introduced to his poetry by <a href="/wiki/W._H._Auden" title="W. H. Auden">W. H. Auden</a>.  They had discussions about collaborating on a "musical theatrical work" for which Thomas would provide the libretto on the theme of "the rediscovery of love and language in what  might be left after the world after the bomb."  The shock of Thomas\'s death later in the year moved Stravinsky to compose his <i>In Memoriam Dylan Thomas</i> for tenor, string quartet, and four trombones. The  first performance in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> in 1954 was introduced with a tribute to Thomas from <a href="/wiki/Aldous_Huxley" title="Aldous Huxley">Aldous Huxley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thomas spent the last nine or ten days of his third tour in New York mostly in the company of Reitell, with whom he had an affair.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris1989p321_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris1989p321-120">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> During this time Thomas fractured his arm falling down a flight of stairs when drunk. Reitell\'s doctor, Milton Feltenstein, put his arm in plaster and treated him for gout and gastritis.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris1989p321_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris1989p321-120">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After returning home, Thomas worked on <i>Under Milk Wood</i> in Wales before sending the original manuscript to Douglas Cleverdon on 15 October 1953. It was copied and returned to Thomas, who lost it in a pub in London and required a duplicate to take to America.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris1989p328_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris1989p328-121">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UMW_Chron_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UMW_Chron-122">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Thomas flew to the States on 19 October 1953 for what would be his final tour.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris1989p328_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris1989p328-121">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> He died in New York before the BBC could record <i>Under Milk Wood</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Burton" title="Richard Burton">Richard Burton</a> starred in the first broadcast in 1954, and was joined by <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Taylor" title="Elizabeth Taylor">Elizabeth Taylor</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Under_Milk_Wood_(1972_film)" title="Under Milk Wood (1972 film)">subsequent film</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> In 1954 the play won the <a href="/wiki/Prix_Italia" title="Prix Italia">Prix Italia</a> for literary or dramatic programmes.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;nb 7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thomas\'s last collection <i>Collected Poems, 1934\xe2\x80\x931952</i>, published when he was 38, won the <a href="/wiki/William_Foyle" title="William Foyle">Foyle</a> poetry prize.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Reviewing the volume, critic <a href="/wiki/Philip_Toynbee" title="Philip Toynbee">Philip Toynbee</a> declared that "Thomas is the greatest living poet in the English language".<sup id="cite_ref-Bold61_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bold61-118">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Thomas\'s father died from pneumonia just before Christmas 1952. In the first few months of 1953 his sister died from liver cancer, one of his patrons took an overdose of sleeping pills, three friends died at an early age and Caitlin had an abortion.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dylan_Thomas%27s_grave_geograph-2755360-by-Gordon-Griffiths.jpg" class="image"><img alt=" A simple white cross engraved with a memorial message to Thomas stands in a grave yard" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Dylan_Thomas%27s_grave_geograph-2755360-by-Gordon-Griffiths.jpg/170px-Dylan_Thomas%27s_grave_geograph-2755360-by-Gordon-Griffiths.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Dylan_Thomas%27s_grave_geograph-2755360-by-Gordon-Griffiths.jpg/255px-Dylan_Thomas%27s_grave_geograph-2755360-by-Gordon-Griffiths.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Dylan_Thomas%27s_grave_geograph-2755360-by-Gordon-Griffiths.jpg/340px-Dylan_Thomas%27s_grave_geograph-2755360-by-Gordon-Griffiths.jpg 2x" data-file-width="368" data-file-height="491" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dylan_Thomas%27s_grave_geograph-2755360-by-Gordon-Griffiths.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thomas\'s grave at the churchyard in <a href="/wiki/Laugharne" title="Laugharne">Laugharne</a>, in 2011</div></div></div>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:380px;&#32;; background-color: #FFFFF0;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9c ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9d ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-quote quoted left-aligned" style=""><div class="poem">\n<p>And death shall have no dominion.<br />\nDead men naked they shall be one<br />\nWith the man in the wind and the west moon;<br />\nWhen their bones are picked clean and the clean bones gone,<br />\nThey shall have stars at elbow and foot;<br />\nThough they go mad they shall be sane,<br />\nThough they sink through the sea they shall rise again<br />\nThough lovers be lost love shall not;<br />\nAnd death shall have no dominion.\n</p>\n</div></div>\n<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">From "<a href="/wiki/And_death_shall_have_no_dominion" title="And death shall have no dominion">And death shall have no dominion</a>"<br /><i>Twenty-five Poems</i> (1936)</cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>Thomas arrived in New York on 20 October 1953 to undertake another tour of poetry-reading and talks, organised by Brinnin.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;nb 8&#93;</a></sup> Although he complained of chest trouble and gout while still in Britain, there is no record that he received medical treatment for either condition.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris1989p315_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris1989p315-131">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;nb 9&#93;</a></sup> He was in a melancholy mood about the trip and his health was poor; he relied on an inhaler to aid his breathing and there were reports that he was suffering from <a href="/wiki/Syncope_(medicine)" title="Syncope (medicine)">blackouts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thomas2008p57_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas2008p57-134">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> His visit to say goodbye to BBC producer <a href="/wiki/Philip_Burton_(theatre_director)" title="Philip Burton (theatre director)">Philip Burton</a>, a few days before he left for New York, was interrupted by a blackout. On his last night in London he had another in the company of his fellow poet Louis MacNeice. The next day he visited a doctor for a smallpox-vaccination certificate.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Plans called for a first appearance at a rehearsal of <i>Under Milk Wood</i> at the Poetry Centre. Brinnin, who was director of the Poetry Centre, did not travel to New York but remained in <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> to write.<sup id="cite_ref-Fatal_Neglect_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fatal_Neglect-136">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> He handed responsibility to his assistant, Liz Reitell, who was keen to see Thomas for the first time since their three-week romance early in the year. She met Thomas at <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_International_Airport" title="John F. Kennedy International Airport">Idlewild Airport</a> and was shocked at his appearance, as he "looked pale, delicate and shaky, not his usual robust self".<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas2008p57_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas2008p57-134">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Thomas told her he had had a terrible week, had missed her terribly and wanted to go to bed with her. Despite Reitell\'s previous misgivings about their relationship, they spent the rest of the day and night together. After being taken by Reitell to check in at the <a href="/wiki/Hotel_Chelsea" title="Hotel Chelsea">Chelsea Hotel</a>, Thomas took the first rehearsal of <i>Under Milk Wood</i>. They then went to the <a href="/wiki/White_Horse_Tavern_(New_York_City)" title="White Horse Tavern (New York City)">White Horse Tavern</a> in Greenwich Village, before returning to the Chelsea Hotel.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The next day Reitell invited him to her apartment, but he declined. They went sight-seeing, but Thomas felt unwell and retired to his bed for the rest of the afternoon. Reitell gave him half a <a href="/wiki/Grain_(unit)" title="Grain (unit)">grain</a> (32.4 milligrams) of <a href="/wiki/Phenobarbitone" class="mw-redirect" title="Phenobarbitone">phenobarbitone</a> to help him sleep and spent the night at the hotel with him. Two days later, on 23 October, Herb Hannum, a friend from an earlier trip, noticed how sick Thomas looked and suggested an appointment with Feltenstein before the performances of <i>Under Milk Wood</i> that evening. Feltenstein administered injections and Thomas made it through the two performances, but collapsed immediately afterwards.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Reitell later said that Feltenstein was "rather a wild doctor who thought injections would cure anything".<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WhiteHorseTavern2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt=" On the corner of a block is a building with large glass fronts on both sides; a sign displaying the tavern&#39;s name shines brightly above in red neon." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/WhiteHorseTavern2008.jpg/220px-WhiteHorseTavern2008.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/WhiteHorseTavern2008.jpg/330px-WhiteHorseTavern2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/WhiteHorseTavern2008.jpg/440px-WhiteHorseTavern2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1783" data-file-height="1193" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WhiteHorseTavern2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The White Horse Tavern, <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Village" title="Greenwich Village">Greenwich Village</a></div></div></div>\n<p>On the evening of 27 October Thomas attended his 39th birthday party but felt unwell and returned to his hotel after an hour.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris1989p332_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris1989p332-140">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The next day he took part in <i>Poetry and the Film</i>, a recorded symposium at <a href="/wiki/Cinema_16" title="Cinema 16">Cinema 16</a>, with panellists <a href="/wiki/Amos_Vogel" title="Amos Vogel">Amos Vogel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Miller" title="Arthur Miller">Arthur Miller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maya_Deren" title="Maya Deren">Maya Deren</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parker_Tyler" title="Parker Tyler">Parker Tyler</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Willard_Maas" title="Willard Maas">Willard Maas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris1989p332_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris1989p332-140">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A turning point came on 2 November. <a href="/wiki/Smog" title="Smog">Air pollution</a> in New York had risen significantly and exacerbated chest illnesses such as Thomas had. By the end of the month over 200 New Yorkers had died from the smog.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas2008p57_134-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas2008p57-134">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> On 3 November, Thomas spent most of the day in bed drinking.<sup id="cite_ref-Alch_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alch-142">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> He went out in the evening to keep two drink appointments. After returning to the hotel he went out again for a drink at 2&#160;am. After drinking at the White Horse, a pub he had found through Scottish poet <a href="/wiki/Ruthven_Todd" title="Ruthven Todd">Ruthven Todd</a>, Thomas returned to the Hotel Chelsea, declaring, "I\'ve had 18 straight whiskies. I think that\'s the record!"<sup id="cite_ref-Alch_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alch-142">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> The barman, and the owner of the pub who served him, later commented that Thomas could not have imbibed more than half that amount.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Thomas had an appointment at a <a href="/wiki/Soft-shell_clam#Cooking" title="Soft-shell clam">clam</a> house in <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> with Todd on 4 November.<sup id="cite_ref-Todd_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Todd-144">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> When phoned at the Chelsea that morning, he said he was feeling ill and postponed the engagement. Later he went drinking with Reitell at the White Horse and, feeling sick again, returned to the hotel.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Feltenstein came to see him three times that day, administering the cortisone secretant <a href="/wiki/ACTH" class="mw-redirect" title="ACTH">ACTH</a> by injection and, on his third visit, half a grain (32.4 milligrams) of <a href="/wiki/Morphine_sulphate" class="mw-redirect" title="Morphine sulphate">morphine sulphate</a>, which affected his breathing. Reitell became increasingly concerned and telephoned Feltenstein for advice. He suggested she get male assistance, so she called upon the painter Jack Heliker, who arrived before 11&#160;pm.<sup id="cite_ref-Todd_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Todd-144">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> At midnight on 5 November Thomas\'s breathing became more difficult and his face turned blue.<sup id="cite_ref-Todd_144-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Todd-144">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> An ambulance was summoned.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;nb 10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thomas was admitted to the emergency ward at <a href="/wiki/Saint_Vincent%27s_Catholic_Medical_Center" title="Saint Vincent&#39;s Catholic Medical Center">St Vincent\'s Hospital</a> at 1:58&#160;am. He was comatose, and his medical notes state that the "impression upon admission was acute <a href="/wiki/Toxic_encephalopathy" title="Toxic encephalopathy">alcoholic encephalopathy</a> damage to the brain by alcohol, for which the patient was treated without response".<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-148">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Caitlin flew to America the following day and was taken to the hospital, by which time a <a href="/wiki/Tracheotomy" title="Tracheotomy">tracheotomy</a> had been performed. Her reported first words were, "Is the bloody man dead yet?"<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-148">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> She was allowed to see Thomas only for 40 minutes in the morning<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> but returned in the afternoon and, in a drunken rage, threatened to kill Brinnin. When she became uncontrollable, she was put in a <a href="/wiki/Straitjacket" title="Straitjacket">straitjacket</a> and committed, by Feltenstein, to the River Crest private psychiatric detox clinic on Long Island.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is also now believed, however, that Thomas had been suffering from <a href="/wiki/Bronchitis" title="Bronchitis">bronchitis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Emphysema" class="mw-redirect" title="Emphysema">emphysema</a>, immediately before his death. In their 2004 book <i>Dylan Remembered 1935-1953, Volume 2,</i> David N. Thomas and Dr Simon Barton disclose that Thomas was found to have pneumonia when he was admitted to hospital in a coma. Doctors took three hours to restore his breathing, using artificial respiration and oxygen. Summarising their findings they conclude: "The medical notes indicate that, on admission, Dylan\'s bronchial disease was found to be very extensive, affecting upper, mid and lower lung fields, both left and right.\xe2\x80\x9d<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Thomas died at noon on 9 November, having never recovered from his coma.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_148-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-148">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-History_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-152">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dylan_Thomas%27s_shed_-_geograph.org.uk_-_15855.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Dylan_Thomas%27s_shed_-_geograph.org.uk_-_15855.jpg/220px-Dylan_Thomas%27s_shed_-_geograph.org.uk_-_15855.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Dylan_Thomas%27s_shed_-_geograph.org.uk_-_15855.jpg/330px-Dylan_Thomas%27s_shed_-_geograph.org.uk_-_15855.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Dylan_Thomas%27s_shed_-_geograph.org.uk_-_15855.jpg/440px-Dylan_Thomas%27s_shed_-_geograph.org.uk_-_15855.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="425" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dylan_Thomas%27s_shed_-_geograph.org.uk_-_15855.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dylan Thomas\'s writing shed in <a href="/wiki/Laugharne" title="Laugharne">Laugharne</a>, largely untouched since his death</div></div></div>\n<p>Rumours circulated of a <a href="/wiki/Brain_haemorrhage" class="mw-redirect" title="Brain haemorrhage">brain haemorrhage</a>, followed by competing reports that he had been mugged and even that he had drunk himself to death.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_148-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-148">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Later, there was speculation about drugs and <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" class="mw-redirect" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>. At the <a href="/wiki/Post-mortem" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-mortem">post-mortem</a>, the pathologist found three causes of death&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 pneumonia, brain swelling and a fatty liver. Despite his heavy drinking his liver showed no sign of <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-History_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-152">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dylan\'s legacy as the "doomed poet" was cemented with the publication of Brinnin\'s 1955 biography <i>Dylan Thomas in America</i>, which focuses on his last few years and paints a picture of him as a drunk and a philanderer.<sup id="cite_ref-Poetry_Foundation_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poetry_Foundation-153">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Later biographies are critical of Brinnin\'s view, especially his coverage of Thomas\'s death. David Thomas in <i>Fatal Neglect: Who Killed Dylan Thomas?</i> claims that Brinnin, along with Reitell and Feltenstein, were culpable.<sup id="cite_ref-Fatal_Neglect_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fatal_Neglect-136">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> FitzGibbon\'s 1965 biography ignores Thomas\'s heavy drinking and skims over his death, giving just two pages in his detailed book to Thomas\'s demise. Ferris in his 1989 biography includes Thomas\'s heavy drinking, but is more critical of those around him in his final days and does not draw the conclusion that he drank himself to death. Feltenstein\'s role and actions have been criticised by many sources, especially his incorrect diagnosis of <a href="/wiki/Delirium_tremens" title="Delirium tremens">delirium tremens</a> and the high dose of morphine he administered.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Dr C. G. de Gutierrez-Mahoney, the doctor who treated Thomas while at St. Vincents, concluded that Feltenstein\'s failure to see that Thomas was gravely ill and have him admitted to hospital sooner, "was even more culpable than his use of morphine".<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Caitlin Thomas\'s autobiographies, <i>Caitlin Thomas&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 Leftover Life to Kill</i> (1957) and <i>My Life with Dylan Thomas: Double Drink Story</i> (1997), describe the destructive effect of alcohol on the poet and to their relationship. "But ours was a drink story, not a love story, just like millions of others.
