Our one and only true love was drink", she wrote<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> and "The bar was our altar".<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Biographer <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Lycett" title="Andrew Lycett">Andrew Lycett</a> ascribed the demise of Thomas\'s health to an alcoholic co-dependent relationship with his wife, who deeply resented his extramarital affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> In contrast Dylan biographers <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Sinclair" title="Andrew Sinclair">Andrew Sinclair</a> and  <a href="/wiki/George_Tremlett" title="George Tremlett">George Tremlett</a> are of the view that Dylan was not an alcoholic.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Tremlett argues that many of his health issues derived from undiagnosed <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" class="mw-redirect" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thomas died <a href="/wiki/Intestacy" title="Intestacy">intestate</a> with assets to the value of \xc2\xa3100.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> His body was brought back to Wales for burial in the village churchyard at Laugharne.<sup id="cite_ref-Centre_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Centre-162">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Thomas\'s funeral, which Brinnin did not attend, took place at St Martin\'s Church in Laugharne on 24 November. Thomas\'s coffin was carried by six friends from the village.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Caitlin, without her customary hat, walked behind the coffin, with his childhood friend <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Jones_(composer)" title="Daniel Jones (composer)">Daniel Jones</a> at her arm and her mother by her side.<sup id="cite_ref-Pathe_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pathe-164">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> The procession to the church was filmed and the wake took place at Brown\'s Hotel.<sup id="cite_ref-Pathe_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pathe-164">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Thomas\'s obituary in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> was written by fellow poet and long-time friend Vernon Watkins.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>His widow, Caitlin, died in 1994 and was buried alongside him.<sup id="cite_ref-Caitlin_obit_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caitlin_obit-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Thomas\'s father "DJ" died on 16 December 1952 and his mother Florence in August 1958. Thomas\'s elder son, Llewelyn, died in 2000, his daughter, Aeronwy in 2009 and his youngest son Colm in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-Centre_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Centre-162">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Poetry">Poetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Poetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poetic_style_and_influences">Poetic style and influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Poetic style and influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Thomas\'s refusal to align with any literary group or movement has made him and his work difficult to categorize.<sup id="cite_ref-Poetry_Foundation_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poetry_Foundation-153">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Although influenced by the modern symbolism and surrealism movement he refused to follow its creed.<sup id="cite_ref-Poetry_Foundation_153-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poetry_Foundation-153">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Instead Thomas is viewed as part of the <a href="/wiki/Modernism" title="Modernism">modernism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">romanticism</a> movements, though attempts to pigeon-hole him within a particular <a href="/wiki/Neo-romanticism" title="Neo-romanticism">neo-romantic</a> school have been unsuccessful.<sup id="cite_ref-Poetry_Foundation_153-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poetry_Foundation-153">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Elder_Olson" title="Elder Olson">Elder Olson</a>, in his 1954 critical study of Thomas\'s poetry, wrote "...&#160;a further characteristic which distinguished Thomas\'s work from that of other poets. It was unclassifiable." Olson continued that in a postmodern age that continually attempted to demand that poetry have social reference, none could be found in Thomas\'s work, and that his work was so obscure that critics could not explicate it.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thomas\'s verbal style played against strict verse forms, such as in the <a href="/wiki/Villanelle" title="Villanelle">villanelle</a> "Do not go gentle into that good night". His images were carefully ordered in a patterned sequence, and his major theme was the unity of all life, the continuing process of life and death and new life that linked the generations. Thomas saw biology as a magical transformation producing unity out of diversity, and in his poetry sought a poetic ritual to celebrate this unity. He saw men and women locked in cycles of growth, love, <a href="/wiki/Procreation" class="mw-redirect" title="Procreation">procreation</a>, new growth, death, and new life. Therefore, each image engenders its opposite. Thomas derived his closely woven, sometimes self-contradictory images from the Bible, <a href="/wiki/Welsh_mythology" title="Welsh mythology">Welsh folklore</a>, preaching, and <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Explaining the source of his imagery, Thomas wrote in a letter to <a href="/wiki/Glyn_Jones_(Welsh_writer)" title="Glyn Jones (Welsh writer)">Glyn Jones</a>: "My own obscurity is quite an unfashionable one, based, as it is, on a preconceived symbolism derived (I\'m afraid all this sounds wooly and pretentious) from the cosmic significance of the human anatomy".<sup id="cite_ref-Poetry_Foundation_153-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poetry_Foundation-153">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:380px;&#32;; background-color: #FFFFF0;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303512"/>\n<div class="quotebox-quote quoted left-aligned" style=""><div class="poem">\n<p>Who once were a bloom of wayside brides in the hawed house<br />\nAnd heard the lewd, wooed field flow to the coming frost,<br />\nThe scurrying, furred small friars squeal in the dowse<br />\nOf day, in the thistle aisles, till the white owl crossed\n</p>\n</div></div>\n<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">From "In the white giant\'s thigh" (1950)<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>Thomas\'s early poetry was noted for its verbal density, <a href="/wiki/Alliteration" title="Alliteration">alliteration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sprung_rhythm" title="Sprung rhythm">sprung rhythm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Internal_rhyme" title="Internal rhyme">internal rhyme</a>, and he was described by some critics as having been influenced by the English poet <a href="/wiki/Gerard_Manley_Hopkins" title="Gerard Manley Hopkins">Gerard Manley Hopkins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WAEoW_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WAEoW-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> This is attributed to Hopkins, who taught himself Welsh and who used sprung verse, bringing some features of Welsh poetic <a href="/wiki/Metre_(poetry)" title="Metre (poetry)">metre</a> into his work.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris(1989)115_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris(1989)115-173">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Henry_Treece" title="Henry Treece">Henry Treece</a> wrote to Thomas comparing his style to that of Hopkins, Thomas wrote back denying any such influence.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris(1989)115_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris(1989)115-173">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> One poet Thomas greatly admired, and who is regarded as an influence, was <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hardy" title="Thomas Hardy">Thomas Hardy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WAEoW_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WAEoW-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferris(1989)259_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris(1989)259-174">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> When Thomas travelled in America, he recited Hardy\'s work in his readings.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris(1989)259_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris(1989)259-174">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other poets from whom critics believe Thomas drew influence include <a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">James Joyce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Rimbaud" title="Arthur Rimbaud">Arthur Rimbaud</a> and <a href="/wiki/D._H._Lawrence" title="D. H. Lawrence">D. H. Lawrence</a>. <a href="/wiki/William_York_Tindall" title="William York Tindall">William York Tindall</a>, in his 1962 study, <i>A Reader\'s Guide to Dylan Thomas</i>, finds comparison between Thomas\'s and Joyce\'s wordplay, while he notes the themes of rebirth and nature are common to the works of Lawrence and Thomas.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;nb 11&#93;</a></sup> Although Thomas described himself as the "Rimbaud of Cwmdonkin Drive", he stated that the phrase "Swansea\'s Rimbaud" was coined by poet <a href="/wiki/Roy_Campbell_(poet)" title="Roy Campbell (poet)">Roy Campbell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;nb 12&#93;</a></sup> Critics have explored the connection between the creation of Thomas\'s mythological pasts into his works such as "The Orchards", which Ann Elizabeth Mayer believes reflects the Welsh myths of the <i>Mabinogion</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-UMW_Chron_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UMW_Chron-122">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;nb 13&#93;</a></sup>\nThomas\'s poetry is notable for its musicality,<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> most clear in "Fern Hill", "In Country Sleep", "Ballad of the Long-legged Bait" and "In the White Giant\'s Thigh" from <i>Under Milk Wood</i>.\n</p><p>Thomas once confided that the poems which had most influenced him were <i>Mother Goose</i> rhymes which his parents taught him when he was a child:\n</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>I should say I wanted to write poetry in the beginning because I had fallen in love with words. The first poems I knew were nursery rhymes and before I could read them for myself I had come to love the words of them. The words alone. What the words stood for was of a very secondary importance&#160;... I fell in love, that is the only expression I can think of, at once, and am still at the mercy of words, though sometimes now, knowing a little of their behaviour very well, I think I can influence them slightly and have even learned to beat them now and then, which they appear to enjoy. I tumbled for words at once. And, when I began to read the nursery rhymes for myself, and, later, to read other verses and ballads, I knew that I had discovered the most important things, to me, that could be ever.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Thomas was an accomplished writer of <a href="/wiki/Prose_poetry" title="Prose poetry">prose poetry</a>, with collections such as <i>Portrait of the Artist as a Young Dog</i> (1940) and <i>Quite Early One Morning</i> (1954) showing he was capable of writing moving short stories.<sup id="cite_ref-WAEoW_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WAEoW-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> His first published prose work was <i>After the Fair</i>, printed in The New English Weekly on 15 March 1934.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Jacob Korg believes that Thomas\'s fiction work can be classified into two main bodies, vigorous fantasies in a poetic style and, after 1939, more straightforward narratives.<sup id="cite_ref-Korg_Prose_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korg_Prose-185">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Korg surmises that Thomas approached his prose writing as an alternate poetic form, which allowed him to produce complex, involuted narratives that do not allow the reader to rest.<sup id="cite_ref-Korg_Prose_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korg_Prose-185">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="As_a_.27Welsh.27_poet"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_\'Welsh\'_poet">As a \'Welsh\' poet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: As a &#039;Welsh&#039; poet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:300px;&#32;; background-color: #FFFFF0;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303512"/>\n<div class="quotebox-quote quoted left-aligned" style=""><div class="poem">\n<p>Not for the proud man apart<br />\nFrom the raging moon I write<br />\nOn these spindrift pages<br />\nNor for the towering dead<br />\nWith their nightingales and psalms<br />\nBut for the lovers, their arms<br />\nRound the griefs of the ages,<br />\nWho pay no praise or wages<br />\nNor heed my craft or art.\n</p>\n</div></div>\n<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">From "<a href="/wiki/In_my_Craft_or_Sullen_Art" title="In my Craft or Sullen Art">In my Craft or Sullen Art</a>" <br /> <i><a href="/wiki/Deaths_and_Entrances" title="Deaths and Entrances">Deaths and Entrances</a></i>, 1946<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>Thomas disliked being regarded as a provincial poet, and decried any notion of \'Welshness\' in his poetry.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris(1989)115_173-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris(1989)115-173">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> When he wrote to <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Spender" title="Stephen Spender">Stephen Spender</a> in 1952, thanking him for a review of his <i>Collected Poems</i>, he added "Oh, &amp; I forgot. I\'m not influenced by Welsh bardic poetry. I can\'t read Welsh."<sup id="cite_ref-Ferris(1989)115_173-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferris(1989)115-173">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Despite this his work was rooted in the geography of Wales. Thomas acknowledged that he returned to Wales when he had difficulty writing, and John Ackerman argues that "His inspiration and imagination were rooted in his Welsh background".<sup id="cite_ref-geoforum_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geoforum-187">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Caitlin Thomas wrote that he worked "in a fanatically narrow groove, although there was nothing narrow about the depth and understanding of his feelings. The groove of direct hereditary descent in the land of his birth, which he never in thought, and hardly in body, moved out of."<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Head of Programmes Wales at the BBC, <a href="/wiki/Aneirin_Talfan_Davies" title="Aneirin Talfan Davies">Aneirin Talfan Davies</a>, who commissioned several of Thomas\'s early radio talks, believed that the poet\'s "whole attitude is that of the medieval bards." <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_O._Morgan" title="Kenneth O. Morgan">Kenneth O. Morgan</a> counter-argues that it is a \'difficult enterprise\' to find traces of <i><a href="/wiki/Cynghanedd" title="Cynghanedd">cynghanedd</a></i> (consonant harmony) or <i><a href="/wiki/Cerdd_dafod" title="Cerdd dafod">cerdd dafod</a></i> (tongue-craft) in Thomas\'s poetry.<sup id="cite_ref-Morgan_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan-190">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Instead he believes his work, especially his earlier more autobiographical poems, are rooted in a changing country which echoes the Welshness of the past and the <a href="/wiki/Anglicisation" title="Anglicisation">Anglicisation</a> of the new industrial nation: "rural and urban, chapel-going and profane, Welsh and English, Unforgiving and deeply compassionate."<sup id="cite_ref-Morgan_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan-190">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Fellow poet and critic <a href="/wiki/Glyn_Jones_(Welsh_writer)" title="Glyn Jones (Welsh writer)">Glyn Jones</a> believed that any traces of <i>cynghanedd</i> in Thomas\'s work were accidental, although he felt Thomas consciously employed one element of Welsh metrics; that of counting syllables per line instead of <a href="/wiki/Foot_(prosody)" title="Foot (prosody)">feet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;nb 14&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Constantine_Fitzgibbon" title="Constantine Fitzgibbon">Constantine Fitzgibbon</a>, Thomas\'s first in-depth biographer, wrote "No major English poet has ever been as Welsh as Dylan".<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although Thomas had a deep connection with Wales, he disliked <a href="/wiki/Welsh_nationalism" title="Welsh nationalism">Welsh nationalism</a>. He once wrote, "<a href="/wiki/Hen_Wlad_Fy_Nhadau" title="Hen Wlad Fy Nhadau">Land of my fathers</a>, and my fathers can keep it".<sup id="cite_ref-FitzGibbon_10_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FitzGibbon_10-194">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> While often attributed to Thomas himself, this line actually comes from the character Owen Morgan-Vaughan, in the screenplay Thomas wrote for the 1948 British melodrama <i><a href="/wiki/The_Three_Weird_Sisters" title="The Three Weird Sisters">The Three Weird Sisters</a></i>. Robert Pocock, a friend from the BBC, recalled "I only once heard Dylan express an opinion on Welsh Nationalism. He used three words. Two of them were Welsh Nationalism."<sup id="cite_ref-FitzGibbon_10_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FitzGibbon_10-194">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Although not expressed as strongly, Glyn Jones believed that he and Thomas\'s friendship cooled in the later years as he had not \'rejected enough\' of the elements that Thomas disliked&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 "Welsh nationalism and a sort of hill farm morality".<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Apologetically, in a letter to <a href="/wiki/Keidrych_Rhys" title="Keidrych Rhys">Keidrych Rhys</a>, editor of literary magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Wales_(magazine)" title="Wales (magazine)">Wales</a></i>, Thomas\'s father wrote that he was "afraid Dylan isn\'t much of a Welshman".<sup id="cite_ref-FitzGibbon_10_194-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FitzGibbon_10-194">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Though FitzGibbon asserts that Thomas\'s negativity towards Welsh nationalism was fostered by his father\'s hostility towards the Welsh language.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_reception">Critical reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Critical reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Thomas\'s work and stature as a poet have been much debated by critics and biographers since his death. Critical studies have been clouded by Thomas\'s personality and mythology, especially his drunken persona and death in New York. When <a href="/wiki/Seamus_Heaney" title="Seamus Heaney">Seamus Heaney</a> gave an <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a> lecture on the poet he opened by addressing the assembly, "Dylan Thomas is now as much a case history as a chapter in the history of poetry", querying how \'Thomas the Poet\' is one of his forgotten attributes.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Holbrook" title="David Holbrook">David Holbrook</a>, who has written three books about Thomas, stated in his 1962 publication <i>Llareggub Revisited</i>, "the strangest feature of Dylan Thomas\'s notoriety\xe2\x80\x94not that he is bogus, but that attitudes to poetry attached themselves to him which not only threaten the prestige, effectiveness and accessibility to English poetry, but also destroyed his true voice and, at last, him."<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Poetry_Archive" title="Poetry Archive">Poetry Archive</a> notes that "Dylan Thomas\'s detractors accuse him of being drunk on language as well as whiskey, but whilst there\'s no doubt that the sound of language is central to his style, he was also a disciplined writer who re-drafted obsessively".<sup id="cite_ref-Poetry_Archive_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poetry_Archive-200">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many critics have argued that Thomas\'s work is too narrow and that he suffers from verbal extravagance.<sup id="cite_ref-Wroe_2003_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wroe_2003-201">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Those that have championed his work have found the criticism baffling. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Lowell" title="Robert Lowell">Robert Lowell</a> wrote in 1947, "Nothing could be more wrongheaded than the English disputes about Dylan Thomas\'s greatness&#160;... He is a dazzling obscure writer who can be enjoyed without understanding."<sup id="cite_ref-LRB_04-03-04_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LRB_04-03-04-202">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Rexroth" title="Kenneth Rexroth">Kenneth Rexroth</a> said, on reading <i>Eighteen Poems</i>, "The reeling excitement of a poetry-intoxicated schoolboy smote the Philistine as hard a blow with one small book as <a href="/wiki/Algernon_Charles_Swinburne" title="Algernon Charles Swinburne">Swinburne</a> had with <i>Poems and Ballads</i>."<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Philip_Larkin" title="Philip Larkin">Philip Larkin</a> in a letter to <a href="/wiki/Kingsley_Amis" title="Kingsley Amis">Kingsley Amis</a> in 1948, wrote that "no one can \'stick words into us like pins\'...&#160;like he [Thomas] can", but followed that by stating that he "doesn\'t use his words to any advantage".<sup id="cite_ref-LRB_04-03-04_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LRB_04-03-04-202">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> Amis was far harsher, finding little of merit in his work, and claiming that he was \'frothing at the mouth with piss.\'<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> In 1956, the publication of the anthology <i>New Lines</i> featuring works by the British collective <a href="/wiki/The_Movement_(literature)" title="The Movement (literature)">The Movement</a>, which included Amis and Larkin amongst its number, set out a vision of modern poetry that was damning towards the poets of the 1940s. Thomas\'s work in particular was criticised. David Lodge, writing about The Movement in 1981 stated "Dylan Thomas was made to stand for everything they detest, verbal obscurity, metaphysical pretentiousness, and romantic rhapsodizing".<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite criticism by sections of academia, Thomas\'s work has been embraced by readers more so than many of his contemporaries, and is one of the few modern poets whose name is recognised by the general public.<sup id="cite_ref-Wroe_2003_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wroe_2003-201">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In 2009, over 18,000 votes were cast in a BBC poll to find the UK\'s favourite poet; Thomas was placed 10th.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Poetry_Season_2009_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Poetry_Season_2009-206">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Several of his poems have passed into the cultural mainstream, and his work has been used by authors, musicians and film and television writers.<sup id="cite_ref-Wroe_2003_201-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wroe_2003-201">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> The BBC Radio programme, <a href="/wiki/Desert_Island_Discs" title="Desert Island Discs">Desert Island Discs</a>, in which guests usually choose their favourite songs, has heard 50 participants select a Dylan Thomas recording.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> John Goodby states that this popularity with the reading public allows Thomas\'s work to be classed as vulgar and common.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodby_Preface_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodby_Preface-208">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> He also cites that despite a brief period during the 1960s when Thomas was considered a cultural icon, that the poet has been marginalized in critical circles due to his exuberance, in both life and work, and his refusal to know his place. Goodby believes that Thomas has been mainly snubbed since the 1970s and has become "...&#160;an embarrassment to twentieth-century poetry criticism"<sup id="cite_ref-Goodby_Preface_208-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodby_Preface-208">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> his work failing to fit standard narratives and is thus ignored rather than studied.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorials">Memorials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Memorials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Dylan_Thomas,_Swansea.JPG" class="image"><img alt="A bronze statue of Thomas in the Maritime Quarter, Swansea" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Statue_of_Dylan_Thomas%2C_Swansea.JPG/220px-Statue_of_Dylan_Thomas%2C_Swansea.JPG" width="220" height="294" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Statue_of_Dylan_Thomas%2C_Swansea.JPG/330px-Statue_of_Dylan_Thomas%2C_Swansea.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Statue_of_Dylan_Thomas%2C_Swansea.JPG/440px-Statue_of_Dylan_Thomas%2C_Swansea.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2436" data-file-height="3254" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Dylan_Thomas,_Swansea.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Thomas in Swansea</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Dylan_Thomas" title="Cultural depictions of Dylan Thomas">Cultural depictions of Dylan Thomas</a></div>\n<p>In Swansea\'s maritime quarter are the <a href="/wiki/Dylan_Thomas_Theatre" title="Dylan Thomas Theatre">Dylan Thomas Theatre</a>, home of the Swansea Little Theatre of which Thomas was once a member, and the former Guildhall built in 1825 and now occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Dylan_Thomas_Centre" title="Dylan Thomas Centre">Dylan Thomas Centre</a>, a literature centre, where exhibitions and lectures are held and setting for the annual Dylan Thomas Festival.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> Outside the centre stands a bronze statue of Thomas, by <a href="/wiki/John_Doubleday" title="John Doubleday">John Doubleday</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Another monument to Thomas stands in <a href="/wiki/Cwmdonkin_Park" title="Cwmdonkin Park">Cwmdonkin Park</a>, one of his favourite childhood haunts, close to his birthplace. The memorial is a small rock in an enclosed garden within the park<sup id="cite_ref-Cwmdonkin_Park_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cwmdonkin_Park-212">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> inscribed with the closing lines from <a href="/wiki/Fern_Hill" title="Fern Hill">Fern Hill</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="poem">\n<p><span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 3em;">Oh as I was young and easy in the mercy of his means</span><br />\n<span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 3em;">Time held me green and dying</span><br />\n<span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 3em;">Though I sang in my chains like the sea.<sup id="cite_ref-Cwmdonkin_Park_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cwmdonkin_Park-212">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</p>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dylan_Thomas_Poets_Corner_Westminster_Abbey,.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Dylan_Thomas_Poets_Corner_Westminster_Abbey%2C.jpg/220px-Dylan_Thomas_Poets_Corner_Westminster_Abbey%2C.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Dylan_Thomas_Poets_Corner_Westminster_Abbey%2C.jpg/330px-Dylan_Thomas_Poets_Corner_Westminster_Abbey%2C.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Dylan_Thomas_Poets_Corner_Westminster_Abbey%2C.jpg/440px-Dylan_Thomas_Poets_Corner_Westminster_Abbey%2C.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dylan_Thomas_Poets_Corner_Westminster_Abbey,.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The plaque, in memory of Thomas, laid in <a href="/wiki/Poets%27_Corner" title="Poets&#39; Corner">Poets\' Corner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Thomas\'s home in Laugharne, the <a href="/wiki/Dylan_Thomas_Boathouse" title="Dylan Thomas Boathouse">Boathouse</a>, is a museum run by Carmarthenshire County Council.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> Thomas\'s writing shed is also preserved.<sup id="cite_ref-Writing_shed_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Writing_shed-91">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> In 2004, the <a href="/wiki/Dylan_Thomas_Prize" title="Dylan Thomas Prize">Dylan Thomas Prize</a> was created in his honour, awarded to the best published writer in English under the age of 30.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> In 2005, the <a href="/wiki/Dylan_Thomas_Screenplay_Award" title="Dylan Thomas Screenplay Award">Dylan Thomas Screenplay Award</a> was established. The prize, administered by the Dylan Thomas Centre, is awarded at the annual Swansea Bay Film Festival. In 1982 a plaque was unveiled in <a href="/wiki/Poets%27_Corner" title="Poets&#39; Corner">Poets\' Corner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> The plaque is also inscribed with the last two lines of <a href="/wiki/Fern_Hill" title="Fern Hill">Fern Hill</a>.\n</p><p>In 2014, to celebrate the centenary of Thomas\'s birth, the <a href="/wiki/British_Council" title="British Council">British Council Wales</a> undertook a year-long programme of cultural and educational works.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Highlights included a touring replica of Thomas\'s work shed, Sir <a href="/wiki/Peter_Blake_(artist)" title="Peter Blake (artist)">Peter Blake</a>\'s exhibition of illustrations based on <i>Under Milk Wood</i> and a 36-hours marathon of readings, which included <a href="/wiki/Michael_Sheen" title="Michael Sheen">Michael Sheen</a> and Sir <a href="/wiki/Ian_McKellen" title="Ian McKellen">Ian McKellen</a> performing Thomas\'s work.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> The Royal Patron of The Dylan Thomas 100 Festival was <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Charles, Prince of Wales">Charles, Prince of Wales</a>, who made a recording of <i>Fern Hill</i> for the event.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_works">List of works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit London: Dent, 1995</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Correspondence">Correspondence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Correspondence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>Ferris, Paul (ed) (2017), <i>Dylan Thomas: The Collected Letters</i>, 2 vols. Introduction by Paul Ferris. London: Weidenfeld &amp; Nicolson</li></ul>\n<dl><dd>Vol I:  1931-1939</dd>\n<dd>Vol II: 1939-1953</dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Posthumous_film_adaptations">Posthumous film adaptations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Posthumous film adaptations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>1972: <i><a href="/wiki/Under_Milk_Wood_(1972_film)" title="Under Milk Wood (1972 film)">Under Milk Wood</a></i>, starring <a href="/wiki/Richard_Burton" title="Richard Burton">Richard Burton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Taylor" title="Elizabeth Taylor">Elizabeth Taylor</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peter_O%27Toole" title="Peter O&#39;Toole">Peter O\'Toole</a></li>\n<li>1987: <i>A Child\'s Christmas in Wales</i>, directed by Don McBrearty.</li>\n<li>1992: <i><a href="/wiki/Rebecca%27s_Daughters" title="Rebecca&#39;s Daughters">Rebecca\'s Daughters</a></i> starring <a href="/wiki/Peter_O%27Toole" title="Peter O&#39;Toole">Peter O\'Toole</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joely_Richardson" title="Joely Richardson">Joely Richardson</a></li>\n<li>2007: <i>Dylan Thomas: A War Films Anthology</i> (DDHE/IWM D23702&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 2006)</li>\n<li>2009: <i>Nadolig Plentyn yng Nghymru/A Child\'s Christmas in Wales</i>, 2009 BAFTA Best Short Film, animation, soundtrack in Welsh and English, Director: Dave Unwin. Extras include filmed comments from <a href="/wiki/Aeronwy_Thomas" title="Aeronwy Thomas">Aeronwy Thomas</a>. 5-016886-088457.</li>\n<li>2014: <i><a href="/wiki/Set_Fire_to_the_Stars" title="Set Fire to the Stars">Set Fire to the Stars</a></i>, with Thomas portrayed by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Celyn_Jones&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Celyn Jones (page does not exist)">Celyn Jones</a> and John Brinnin by <a href="/wiki/Elijah_Wood" title="Elijah Wood">Elijah Wood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>2016: <i><a href="/wiki/Dominion_(2016_film)" title="Dominion (2016 film)">Dominion</a></i>, written and directed by <a href="/wiki/Steven_Bernstein_(Director/Cinematographer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Steven Bernstein (Director/Cinematographer)">Steven Bernstein</a>, examines Thomas\' final hours. Thomas is portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Rhys_Ifans" title="Rhys Ifans">Rhys Ifans</a>.</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opera_adaptation">Opera adaptation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Opera adaptation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><i>Unter dem Milchwald</i> by German composer <a href="/wiki/Walter_Steffens_(composer)" title="Walter Steffens (composer)">Walter Steffens</a> on his own libretto using <a href="/wiki/Erich_Fried" title="Erich Fried">Erich Fried</a>\'s translation of <i><a href="/wiki/Under_Milk_Wood" title="Under Milk Wood">Under Milk Wood</a></i> into German, <a href="/wiki/Hamburg_State_Opera" title="Hamburg State Opera">Hamburg State Opera</a> 1973 and <a href="/wiki/Staatstheater_Kassel" title="Staatstheater Kassel">Staatstheater Kassel</a> 1977</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a Trail">Dylan Thomas Trail</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/St._Vincent_(musician)" title="St. Vincent (musician)">St. Vincent</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Footnotes">Footnotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Footnotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In his 1989 biography of Thomas, Ferris claims that two of Thomas\'s friends had stated that they met him in London in 1932, though his late 1933 visit to the city is the first for which evidence exists.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Davenport was, for many years, literary editor of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> newspaper. "From July to November 1940 Dylan Thomas and his family stayed at \'The Malting House\' 78 High Street, Marshfield, near Chippenham in Gloucestershire, with the critic John Davenport and his American painter wife, Clement, who kept an open house for musicians and writers. The composers Lennox Berkeley and Arnold Cooke, the music critic William Glock and writer Antonia White, joined them."<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The reason for being graded unsuitable for military service is vague. His mother said it was due to "punctured lungs", while Vernon Watkins believed it was "scarred" lungs. Neither statement is corroborated by Thomas\'s autopsy, although Milton Helpern found some <a href="/wiki/Emphysema" class="mw-redirect" title="Emphysema">emphysema</a>, probably caused by chain-smoking.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The footage was taken from Riefenstahl\'s 1935 propaganda film <i><a href="/wiki/Triumph_of_the_Will" title="Triumph of the Will">Triumph des Willens</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">John Brinnin in his 1956 book, <i>Dylan Thomas in America</i> (p. 104) states that on a visit to Laugharne in 1951 he was shown "more than two hundred separate and distinct versions of the poem (<i>Fern Hill</i>)" by Thomas.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-99"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-99">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">FitzGibbon, in his 1965 biography, lists 39 venues visited in the first U.S. trip, compiled with the help of John Brinnin, but accepts that some locations may have been missed.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-127"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-127">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The BBC submitted the play posthumously along with a French translation by <a href="/wiki/Jacques-Bernard_Brunius" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacques-Bernard Brunius">Jacques-Bernard Brunius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-130">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although both agree that he left London on 19 October, biographers Ferris and FitzGibbon disagree on his arrival date. Ferris in his 1989 work gives Thomas\'s arrival in New York as 19 October (p. 329) while FitzGibbon writing in 1965 states 20 October (p. 391).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-132">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">David N. Thomas (2008) writes: "He knew that he should see a doctor but feared that he would be pronounced unfit and the trip cancelled". Thomas was financially committed to going.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-147"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-147">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ruthven Todd states in his letter dated 23 November that the police were called, who then called the ambulance, while Ferris in his 1989 biography writes that Feltenstein was summoned again and called the ambulance. D. N. Thomas concurs that Feltenstein eventually returned at 1&#160;am and summoned the ambulance, although it took nearly another hour to get him admitted to the hospital.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-176"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-176">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In reply to a student\'s questions in 1951, Thomas stated: "I do not think that Joyce has had any hand at all in my writing; certainly his <i>Ulysses</i> has not. On the other hand, I cannot deny on the shaping of some of my <i>Portrait</i> stories might owe something to Joyce\'s stories in the volume, <i>Dubliners</i>. But then <i>Dubliners</i> was a pioneering work in the world of the short story, and no good storywriter since can have failed, in some way, however little, to have benefited by it." FitzGibbon (1965), p. 370</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-179"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-179">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In his notes to page 186, Ferris (1989) states that in a <a href="/wiki/BBC_Home_Service" title="BBC Home Service">BBC Home Service</a> programme aired in 1950, <i>Poetic Licence</i>, in which Campbell and Thomas appeared, Thomas said "I won\'t forgive you for the Swansea\'s Rimbaud, because you called me that first Roy".</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-181"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-181">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The Orchard" makes reference to the \'Black Book of Llareggub\'. Here Thomas makes links with religion and the mythic Wales of the <a href="/wiki/White_Book_of_Rhydderch" title="White Book of Rhydderch">White Book of Rhydderch</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Book_of_Carmarthen" title="Black Book of Carmarthen">Black Book of Carmarthen</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-192"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-192">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jones notes that in Thomas\'s early work, such as <i>Eighteen Poems</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Iamb_(foot)" class="mw-redirect" title="Iamb (foot)">iambic foot</a> was the rhythmic basis of his line, while in his later work a count of syllables replaced a count of <a href="/wiki/Accent_(poetry)" title="Accent (poetry)">accents</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dylan_Thomas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; -webkit-column-width: 20em; column-width: 20em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.bbc.co.uk/arts/0/24748894">"Did hard-living or medical neglect kill Dylan Thomas?"</a>. <i>BBC</i>. 8 November 2013<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">20 August</span> 2016</span>. <q>Florence was fiercely proud of her son\'s achievements and was desperately keen to protect her son. This did have its disadvantages. A friend of Dylan\'s, Tom Warner describes Dylan\'s first trip to his house, "the first time Dylan came, we noticed that he was just sitting in rather a helpless way with his egg untouched, and by general gestures we realised he wanted someone to take the top off for him-he\'d never done it himself". Years later, his wife Caitlin would remove the tops off his eggs and would prepare him sugared bread and milk cut neatly into squares when he was ill, just as mam would have done. Despite her overindulgence, she had a strong bond with her children.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Discover+Dylan+Thomas&amp;rft.atitle=Discover+Dylan+Thomas%27s+Life%3A+Mother&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.discoverdylanthomas.com%2Flife%23&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADylan+Thomas" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Janes, Hilly (2014). <i>The Three Lives of Dylan Thomas</i>.
